The Opponent's Claim: Names from Revelation Only
Opponent argues Murji'a and Khawarij agree names from revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the names 'faith' and 'disbelief' can only be derived from revelation, not language, and why this matters in the debate about major sins.

Core Idea
Some argued that naming faith is purely revelational, not linguistic.

Before we examine Maturidi's refutation, we need to hear the argument he is refuting. An opponent claimed that both the Murji'a and Khawarij agreed that the names 'believer' and 'unbeliever' come only from revelation, not from language or analogy. This claim, if true, would have serious implications for how we judge those who commit major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the opponent's claim and the reasoning he uses.
The opponent is likely al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili.
2
Identify the three pieces of the argument
The opponent asserts: (1) Murji'a and Khawarij agree on this principle, (2) the name of faith is not from analogy but from revelation, (3) therefore naming requires tawatur or ijma'. Find each part.
3
Consider the implications
Ask yourself: If names are only from revelation, what happens to the status of a major sinner? How might this affect the debate on faith and deeds?
This will prepare you for Maturidi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج في نفي اسم الكفر والإيمان عن صاحب الكبيرة أن المرجئة والخوارج اتفقوا على أن اسم الإيمان لا يوجد بالقياس مما أوجبته اللغة وإنما كان من جهة السمع، فلا تجوز التسمية بواحد إلا بالتواتر بالسمع أو الإجماع ، وزعم أن هذه حجة كافية.
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق: كذب في الحكاية عمن ذلك، بل هم أجمعوا على تحقيق الاسم بما أوجبته اللغة. لكن الخوارج استدلت بارتكاب الكبيرة على كذبه فيها أظهر من التصديق بقوله : ألم. أحسب الناس الآية . والمرجئة زعمت أن الاستدلال لظهور الصدق والكذب في الغالب لا أن التصديق في الحقيقة لا يكون دونه، والإيمان هو ذلك التصديق في الحقيقة لو كان [و] ليس في الكبيرة نفيه ولا في اتقائها تحقيقه في حق الوجود، وإن كان في حق الدلالة على المستدل. والله أعلم. فحصل إذا القول منهم على ما توجبه اللغة، وظهر كذبه في الحكاية .
Then he argued in negating the name of disbelief and faith from the committer of a major sin that the Murji'a and Khawarij agreed that the name of faith is not derived by analogy from what language necessitates, but rather it is from revelation, so it is not permissible to name by any of them except by frequent transmission or consensus, and he claimed that this is a sufficient proof.
The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah is success: He lied in the report about them; rather, they all agreed on affirming the name according to what language necessitates. But the Khawarij used the commission of a major sin as evidence that his sincerity in it is clearer than his sincerity in his statement: 'Alif Lam Meem? Do people think...' (the verse). And the Murji'a claimed that the evidence is for the apparent truthfulness or falsehood in most cases, not that true assent (al-taṣdīq) in reality does not occur without it, and faith is that true assent in reality even if [it is] not negated by the major sin nor realized by avoiding it with respect to existence, although it may be with respect to indication for the one who infers. And Allah knows best. So the statement from them turned out to be according to what language necessitates, and his lie in the report became clear.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a refutation. The opponent (al-Ka'bi) claimed that the Murji'a and Khawarij both agree that the names 'belief' and 'unbelief' are based on revelation, not language, so no one can be named a believer or unbeliever without textual evidence or consensus. Maturidi refutes this by saying the opponent lied in his report. In reality, both groups affirmed the name based on language. The Khawarij argued that committing a major sin shows that the person's falsehood (kadhib) is more apparent than his truthfulness (taṣdīq), so they deem him an unbeliever. The Murji'a argued that evidence of truthfulness or falsehood is based on apparent actions in most cases, but true assent (al-taṣdīq) is the reality of faith and is not negated by a major sin nor achieved by avoiding it in terms of actual existence, though it may be indicated by actions. Thus, Maturidi concludes that their statements agree with language, and the opponent's claim is false.

Key Terms
المرجئة
الخوارج
اسم الإيمان
السمع
القياس
التواتر
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if names are from revelation, then no one can be called a believer or unbeliever without a direct text.
Correction: The opponent does claim this, but Maturidi will show that the Murji'a and Khawarij actually use linguistic bases.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent, what is the source of the name 'faith'?
A) Language and analogy
B) Revelation (sam‘) only
C) Reason and consensus
D) The deeds of the person

Key Takeaway
The opponent claims that Murji'a and Khawarij concur that 'faith' is a revelational term, not a linguistic one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone a believer or an unbeliever, do you rely on clear revelation or your own judgment? The opponent argued that such naming requires consensus or repeated transmission—but do you apply that standard in your heart, or do you judge based on biases?
?
Both the Murji'a and Khawarij agreed on one point, yet both were deviant. What does that teach you about relying on agreement as proof of truth? Where in your life have you followed a consensus without questioning its foundation?
?
The opponent claimed his argument was 'sufficient proof.' Have you ever been quick to accept an argument because it seemed strong, without checking its premises? What premise in this argument might be false, and how does that challenge your own approach to theological reasoning?

Maturidi's Refutation: The True Positions of Murji'a and Khawarij
Maturidi refutes opponent's claim and explains actual positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the opponent's claim by showing that both the Murji'a and Khawarij actually base their naming on linguistic considerations, not purely on revelation.

Core Idea
Maturidi says: They all agreed on linguistic naming, but differed on evidence.

Now we turn to Maturidi's powerful refutation. He directly accuses the opponent of lying about what the Murji'a and Khawarij actually hold. Then he explains their real positions: both groups use language as a basis for naming, but they differ on how they interpret the evidence from actions. This passage reveals Maturidi's method of defending orthodoxy by correcting historical misrepresentations.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's opening accusation
Notice the strong language: 'كذب في الحكاية' (he lied in the report). This sets the tone for refutation.
2
Understand the true agreement: linguistic naming
Maturidi says both groups agree on 'تحقيق الاسم بما أوجبته اللغة' (affirming the name according to what language necessitates). Find this phrase.
This directly contradicts the opponent's claim.
3
Contrast the Khawarij and Murji'a positions
See how each sect uses actions as evidence: Khawarij argue major sin shows kufr, Murji'a argue it does not negate iman. Maturidi explains their reasoning.
Both use linguistic principles, but arrive at different conclusions.
4
Conclude with Maturidi's verdict
Read the final sentence: 'فظهر كذبه في الحكاية' (his lie in the report became clear). The refutation is complete.

Source Text

According to Maturidi, what is the true common ground between Murji'a and Khawarij?
A) They both deny the importance of deeds
B) They both affirm the name based on language

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at someone who has committed a major sin, do you quietly consider them a 'lost cause'? How does Maturidi's explanation of the Murji'a view—that true assent remains unaffected—challenge your tendency to judge based on outward behavior alone? What would it take for you to leave their ultimate state to Allah while still acting on the indicators of deeds?
?
The Khawarij used a sin as definite proof of falsehood. Are there people in your life—a family member, a leader, a fellow Muslim—whom you have mentally written off as insincere because of a specific failing? Can you today separate your assessment of their apparent indications from your certainty about their inner taṣdīq, which only Allah knows?
?
This lesson reveals that both sects grounded their positions in language, not revelation. Yet we often use verses out of context to label others. Ask yourself: In your heart, do you prioritize linguistic and rational principles when forming opinions about others' faith, or do you rely on selected texts to confirm your preconceptions? Will you commit to studying divine revelation more holistically before passing judgment?

The Early Ummah's Two Positions
Maturidi presents the two early Ummah positions and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions of the early Ummah regarding the grave sinner and the analogy used to explain the dual naming.

Core Idea
The Ummah used two names to capture both faith and sinfulness in the grave sinner.

Before the Mu'tazila emerged, the early Muslim community held two views about the person who commits a major sin. Both views used two names—one indicating belief or unbelief, and another indicating sinfulness. Maturidi provides an analogy to show how such dual naming helps clarify degrees of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two positions: 'believer who is a sinner' and 'unbeliever who is a sinner'.
The Arabic uses 'mujrim fāsiq' etc.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage states the Ummah had two positions on the grave sinner. Find the exact phrases and note that both include two names.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi gives the analogy of 'forbidden that is disliked' and 'lawful that is disliked'. Think about how this shows degrees of prohibition and lawfulness.
The analogy clarifies that the prohibition is not absolute and the lawful is not the preferred kind.
4
Apply the analogy to the Ummah
Maturidi says 'so likewise is the matter of the Ummah'. Reflect on how the dual naming in the positions serves a similar clarifying purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأمة كانت قبل حدوث الاعتزال وأهله على قولين في صاحب الكبيرة: على أنه مؤمن فاسق، أو كافر فاسق، ليُعلم وجه كفره عند من يراه كافرا، ووجه فسقه عند من يراه فاسقا. وذلك كما يقال : حرام مكروه وحلال مكروه، ليعلم أن الحرمة هي بيان أخف الحرمتين، وهي حرمة الكراهة لا حرمة الإطلاق، وأن الحلّ ليس هو حل الإيثار والرغبة، بل فيه بعض ما يورث الشُّبَه ، فمثله أمر الأمة فيما ذكرت. ثم أسقطت المعتزلة أحد الاسمين وهو الذي يعرف معنى التنازع، وألزم الآخر على الاتفاق في منع ذلك مطلقا دون معرفة حقيقته، فخالفوا بذلك الأمة. وهذه حجة مقنعة لمن تصح انيته]. الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the Ummah, before the emergence of the Mu'tazila and its people, had two positions concerning the committer of a major sin: that he is a believer who is a sinner, or an unbeliever who is a sinner, so that the aspect of his unbelief is known according to the one who considers him an unbeliever, and the aspect of his sinfulness is known according to the one who considers him a sinner. This is similar to when one says: forbidden that is disliked, and lawful that is disliked, so that it is known that the prohibition is the explanation of the lesser of the two prohibitions, which is the prohibition of dislike, not the absolute prohibition, and that the lawful is not the lawful of preference and desire, but rather it contains some elements that cause doubts. So likewise is the matter of the Ummah concerning what I mentioned. Then the Mu'tazila dropped one of the two names, which is the one that indicates the meaning of conflict, and imposed the other on agreement, absolutely preventing that without knowledge of its reality, thus they contradicted the Ummah. And this is a convincing proof for one whose intention is sound. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the historical consensus of the early Ummah. Maturidi states that before the Mu'tazila appeared, the Ummah had only two positions regarding the grave sinner: either he is a 'believer who is a sinner' (mu'min fāsiq) or an 'unbeliever who is a sinner' (kāfir fāsiq). Either way, both names (belief/unbelief and sinfulness) were used. He gives an analogy: saying 'forbidden that is disliked' and 'lawful that is disliked' clarifies that there are degrees—the prohibition is not absolute but lesser, and the lawful is not the preferred kind. Similarly, the Ummah used both names to clarify the complexity. The Mu'tazila then dropped one name (the one that indicates conflict) and imposed the other absolutely without understanding its reality, thus violating the way of the Ummah. Maturidi concludes this is a convincing proof for one with sound intention.

Key Terms
الأمة
صاحب الكبيرة
مؤمن فاسق
كافر فاسق
حرام مكروه
حلال مكروه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early Ummah had only one position on the grave sinner.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states there were two positions, both using dual names.

Checkpoint
What were the two positions of the early Ummah regarding the grave sinner?
A) Believer or unbeliever only.
B) Believer who is a sinner, or unbeliever who is a sinner.
C) Sinner or non-sinner.
D) Mu'min or kāfir absolutely.

Key Takeaway
The early Ummah used two names for the grave sinner to acknowledge both faith and sinfulness, analogous to degrees of prohibition and lawfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own soul, do you see yourself only as a 'believer' or only as a 'sinner'? How does the early Ummah's use of two names for the grave sinner challenge you to hold both truths about yourself—your faith and your failures—without dismissing either?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed or struggle with. Does your self-perception lean toward 'I am still a believer' or 'I am practically an unbeliever'? Which of the two positions do you lean toward, and what does that reveal about your understanding of God's mercy and justice?
?
The analogy in this lesson uses 'forbidden that is disliked' to show degrees. In what area of your life do you tend to treat something as absolutely forbidden (or absolutely permissible) when it actually lies in a 'disliked' or 'doubtful' category? How can you recalibrate your conscience to reflect the nuance the early Ummah taught?

The Mu'tazila's Departure
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for dropping one name and calls it a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila departed from the early Ummah's consensus by dropping one of the two names and why Maturidi considers this a proof against them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila innovated by dropping one name, contradicting the Ummah's balanced approach.

In this passage, Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for abandoning the dual naming used by the early Ummah. He argues that they arbitrarily dropped one of the two names and imposed the other absolutely, without understanding the reality behind it. This departure serves as a convincing proof for those with sound intentions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the actions of the Mu'tazila: they dropped one name and imposed the other absolutely.
The Arabic uses 'أسقطت المعتزلة أحد الاسمين وألزم الآخر'.
2
Identify the innovation
Notice that the Mu'tazila removed the name that indicates conflict (the one that acknowledges both aspects). They enforced the other name without considering the nuance.
3
Understand the proof
Maturidi says this is a convincing proof for one with sound intention. Reflect on why their departure from the Ummah's practice is a proof of their error.
The proof lies in their contradiction of the consensus of the Ummah.
4
Apply the lesson
Think about how this argument shows the importance of adhering to the understanding of the early community in theological matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the Mu'tazila dropped one of the two names, which is the one that indicates the meaning of conflict, and imposed the other on agreement, absolutely preventing that without knowledge of its reality, thus they contradicted the Ummah. And this is a convincing proof for one whose intention is sound. And there is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the historical consensus of the early Ummah. Maturidi states that before the Mu'tazila appeared, the Ummah had only two positions regarding the grave sinner: either he is a 'believer who is a sinner' (mu'min fāsiq) or an 'unbeliever who is a sinner' (kāfir fāsiq). Either way, both names (belief/unbelief and sinfulness) were used. How did the Mu'tazila depart from the early Ummah's position?
A) They dropped one name and absolutely imposed the other.
B) They added a third name.
C) They completely agreed with the Ummah.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's innovation was dropping one of the two names used by the Ummah, which serves as a proof of their error for those with sound intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own spiritual state, do you tend to label yourself either fully 'believer' or fully 'sinner', or do you hold both names in tension? How might the Mu'tazila's error of dropping one name be reflected in your tendency to oversimplify your own faith? What would it look like for you to embrace both names today, acknowledging both your belief and your shortcomings before Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila imposed one name absolutely, without knowledge of its reality. In your interactions with others, do you ever judge them absolutely based on one label (e.g., 'sinner', 'hypocrite') while ignoring the complexity of their state? How can this passage compel you to see others—and yourself—with the balanced perspective that the early Ummah maintained?

6 min
The Two Opinions on the Major Sinner
Maturidi presents the two pre-Mu'tazila opinions and explains them with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two early Islamic opinions regarding the major sinner and how their dual naming reflects nuanced judgments.

Core Idea
The early community used two names for the major sinner to capture both faith and sin.

Before the Mu'tazila emerged, the Muslim community had two distinct positions on the status of a person who commits a major sin. Some called such a person a 'believing sinner' while others called him an 'unbelieving sinner.' This lesson explores the reasoning behind these two labels and how they reflect a balanced understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the general meaning. Note the key terms.
2
Understand the two opinions
Identify the two opinions: 'believing sinner' and 'unbelieving sinner'. Notice that each combines a positive and negative attribute.
This dual naming is intentional.
3
Analyze the analogy
Maturidi uses an analogy from fiqh: 'forbidden and disliked' and 'permissible and disliked'. See how this shows a hierarchy of rulings.
4
Connect the analogy to the major sinner
Reflect on how the analogy applies: just as 'forbidden and disliked' indicates a lighter prohibition, 'believing sinner' indicates a partial sinfulness.
The dual name reveals the underlying tension.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأمة كانت قبل حدوث الاعتزال وأهله على قولين في صاحب الكبيرة: على أنه مؤمن فاسق، أو كافر فاسق، ليُعلم وجه كفره عند من يراه كافرا، ووجه فسقه عند من يراه فاسقا. وذلك كما يقال : حرام مكروه وحلال مكروه، ليعلم أن الحرمة هي بيان أخف الحرمتين، وهي حرمة الكراهة لا حرمة الإطلاق، وأن الحلّ ليس هو حل الإيثار والرغبة، بل فيه بعض ما يورث الشُّبَه ، فمثله أمر الأمة فيما ذكرت. ثم أسقطت المعتزلة أحد الاسمين وهو الذي يعرف معنى التنازع، وألزم الآخر على الاتفاق في منع ذلك مطلقا دون معرفة حقيقته، فخالفوا بذلك الأمة. وهذه حجة مقنعة لمن تصح [نيته] الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عارض نفسه بأنك أتبعت الاسم الأحكام، هلا فرّقت بين أصحاب الكبائر بما اختلفت أحكامها ؟ قال : أفعل فأسمى هذا سارقاً وهذا قاذفا. فيقال: فإذ سميته فاسقا مع الذي هو تحقيق اسمه الذي به فسق ولم تمنع عنه اسم فعله، فما بالك منعت عن المؤمن اسم الإيمان له مستحق بفعله ؟ إلا أنك تُوسع التسمية بما يقبح من الفعل وتمنع بما يحسن، وذلك جور في الفعل. ثم اسم الفسق لم يجب بجلد الثمانين ولكن بالموالاة والتعظيم. وهذا يبين الحيد عن تقدير المعارض له، وإنما أراد - والله أعلم - أن الأسماء لم تقدر عن الأحكام، بوجود الاختلاف في الأحكام على الاستواء في الأسماء، وبذلك كان تقديرها في الابتداء. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the community before the emergence of Mu'tazila and its people had two opinions concerning the committer of a major sin: that he is a believing sinner, or an unbelieving sinner, so that the aspect of his disbelief be known to those who consider him an unbeliever, and the aspect of his sinfulness be known to those who consider him a sinner. And that is like when it is said: forbidden and disliked, and permissible and disliked, so that it be known that the prohibition is the expression of the lighter of the two prohibitions, which is the prohibition of dislike, not the absolute prohibition, and that the permissibility is not the permissibility of preference and desire, but rather in it there is something that begets doubts. So similar is the matter of the community regarding what I mentioned. Then the Mu'tazila dropped one of the two names—which is the one that indicates the meaning of conflict—and imposed the other upon agreement, absolutely preventing that without knowing its reality, and thereby they opposed the community. And this is a convincing proof for those whose intention is sound to Allah. And there is no power except through Allah. Then he objected to himself: 'You have attached rulings to the name, why did you not differentiate among the committers of major sins according to the differences in their rulings?' He said: 'I do, so I call this one a thief and that one a slanderer.' So it is said: 'Since you have called him a sinner with the verification of his name by which he sinned, and you did not withhold from him the name of his action, why then do you withhold from the believer the name of faith, which he deserves by his action? Rather, you extend naming to what is ugly from action and withhold from what is good, and that is injustice in action. Then the name of sinfulness is not necessitated by the eighty lashes, but by [showing] loyalty and magnifying [the sinner].' And this shows the deviation from the estimation of his opponent. And he intended—and Allah knows best—that names were not estimated according to rulings, with the presence of difference in rulings despite equality in names, and by that was their estimation in the beginning. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. Maturidi first describes the two positions of the early community before the rise of Mu'tazila regarding the major sinner: either a 'believing sinner' or an 'unbelieving sinner'. This dual naming is explained by analogy to terms like 'forbidden-disliked' or 'permissible-disliked', where the combination indicates a nuanced meaning. The purpose is to clarify that the terms 'kufr' and 'fisq' when applied to the same person are not absolute but carry specific connotations. Then Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for dropping one of the two names (i.e., ignoring the tension) and imposing the other (e.g., absolute 'fisq') without understanding the reality, thereby opposing the consensus. He then presents a self-objection: the opponent might ask why he did not differentiate between different major sins (like theft and slander) since they have different legal punishments. The answer is that while one can distinguish by specific actions (e.g., calling a thief 'thief' and a slanderer 'slanderer'), the general name 'sinner' (fasiq) applies to all because of the common element of sinfulness. But if one withholds the name 'believer' from a major sinner while granting the general name 'sinner', that is inconsistent because the sinner's good actions would still merit the name 'believer'. Maturidi concludes that names are not derived from specific legal rulings but from the inherent reality of the actions, and the difference in rulings does not affect the uniformity of names.

Key Terms
مؤمن فاسق
كافر فاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the early community had a single unified view on the major sinner.
Correction: Maturidi shows there were two views, both recognizing a tension between faith and sin, unlike the later Mu'tazila who simplified.

Checkpoint
What were the two opinions on the major sinner before Mu'tazila?
A) Believing sinner and unbelieving sinner
B) Pure believer and pure sinner
C) Sinner only and believer only
D) Munafiq and kafir

Key Takeaway
The early community used dual names for the major sinner to capture both his faith and his sin, showing a nuanced understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone committing a major sin, do you immediately write them off as a complete unbeliever? What does this passage teach you about the need to balance judgment with mercy?
?
Have you ever been quick to label someone as 'evil' without acknowledging the faith they still hold? How can you apply the early community's nuanced approach in your own dealings?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed. How do you describe yourself in relation to that sin? Do you see yourself as a 'believing sinner' or do you let the sin define your entire identity? What would change if you used the dual naming?

Mu'tazila's Error and the Argument About Names
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazila and defends the dual naming through a self-objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila for dropping one of the two names for the major sinner and his response to the objection that different major sins should have different names.

Core Idea
Names reflect underlying reality, not legal rulings; dropping a name distorts truth.

Having presented the two early opinions, Maturidi now targets the Mu'tazila who, according to him, dropped one of the two names and imposed the other absolutely. He then anticipates an objection: if names follow rulings, why not differentiate between different major sins? His answer clarifies the relationship between names, actions, and rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full argument.
2
Identify Mu'tazila's error
Maturidi says they dropped one name (the one showing tension) and used the other absolutely.
This is a key criticism.
3
Analyze the self-objection
The hypothetical opponent asks: if you attach rulings to names, why not differentiate theft vs. slander?
This tests consistency.
4
Understand Maturidi's response
He says you can call a thief a thief, but the general name 'sinner' applies to all. Withholding 'believer' is unjust.
The response hinges on the principle that names are not derived from legal punishments.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi states that names are not estimated by rulings because rulings differ while names remain the same.
This is the final point.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
8 min
Faith does not vanish with loss of justice
Maturidi uses Qur'anic verses and consensus to prove faith remains with injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Qur'anic verses and the practice of the community to prove that faith (iman) does not disappear when a believer commits sins or loses justice ('adala).

Core Idea
Faith remains even when justice is lost; the Qur'an itself distinguishes between believers and the just.

This passage from Kitab al-Tawhid presents a key Maturidi argument against the Mu'tazila claim that faith and justice are inseparable. Imam al-Maturidi cites four Qur'anic verses and then uses the community's consensus on witness examination to show that a believer may be unjust yet still be called a believer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scriptural evidence
Read the four verses cited and note the logical implication: if every believer were just, the condition of 'just' would be redundant. The verses show that believers can be just or unjust.
The verses are from al-Ma'ida, al-Baqara, al-Talaq, and al-Nisa'.
2
Explore the reductio ad absurdum
Consider what would follow if the Mu'tazila position were true: testimony would be accepted solely on faith without investigating moral character, which contradicts actual practice.
3
Recognize the consensus argument
The community's practice of examining witnesses' moral conditions (ijma') proves that faith and justice are distinct. Faith itself does not negate sins.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the scriptural and consensus evidence to conclude that the name of faith remains even when justice is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن اسم الإيمان لا يزول بزوال العدالة قوله تعالى: ﴿ يا أيها الذين آمنوا شهادة بينكم إذا حضر أحدكم الموت حين الوصية اثنان ذوا عدل منكم ﴾ [المائدة: ١٠٦/٥] الآية، ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلاً لكان يقول: اثنان منكم، إذ كان ابتداء الآية في مخاطبة المؤمنين. ثبت أن قد يكون مؤمناً عدلاً وغير عدل. وقال الله تعالى: ﴿ يا أيها الذين آمنوا إذا تداينتم بدين إلى قوله ممن ترضون من الشهداء ﴾ [البقرة: ۲۸۲/۲] الآية، فلو كان كل مؤمن مرضياً لم يكن للشرط فائدة. وكذلك قوله: ﴿ وأشهدوا ذوي عدل منكم ﴾ [الطلاق: ٢/٦٥] الآية. ثبت أن المؤمن يكون عدلاً وغير عدل. وكذلك قوله: ﴿ فإن آنستم منهم رشدا فادفعوا إليهم أموالهم ﴾ [النساء: ٦/٤] الآية، ثبت أنه قد يكون منهم رشيد وغير رشيد. ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلاً وكل من ليس بعدل ليس بمؤمن لكان لا شهادة تُرَدّ بالفسق بعد الامتحان ولا يجوز السؤال عن الأحوال ليعلم بها العدالة والفسق؛ بل على المكان الذي يُسأل عما عليه من الإيمان ويمكنه الوفاء في ذلك، فيجب قبول شهادته بلا سؤال عنه ولا اعتبار بأحواله. وفي إجماع الأمة على الفحص عن الأحوال وترك النزول على ما يظهر من الأقوال - التي تكتفى بها فيما كان شرائطها الإيمان من الحل والحرمة والتوارث ثم العبادات - دليل يبيّن أن الإيمان وما به يصير المرء مؤمناً ويستوجب أحكامه ليس هو كل ما ينفي أنواع الفسوق والعصيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ذلك مضى أمر هذه الأمة من
The proof that the name of faith does not vanish with the loss of justice is God Almighty's saying: 'O you who have believed, when death approaches one of you at the time of bequest, let there be two just persons among you as witnesses' [al-Ma'ida: 106/5] — the verse. If every believer were just, He would have said 'two from among you', while the beginning of the verse addresses the believers. So it is established that a believer may be just or not just. And God Almighty said: 'O you who have believed, when you contract a debt for a stated term, to the end of the verse: from among those you approve as witnesses' [al-Baqara: 282/2] — the verse. If every believer were approved, the condition would have no benefit. And likewise His saying: 'And call to witness two just persons among you' [al-Talaq: 2/65] — the verse. So it is established that the believer may be just or not just. And likewise His saying: 'And if you perceive from them maturity (rushd), then give them their property' [al-Nisa: 6/4] — the verse. So it is established that there may be among them mature and not mature. And if every believer were just and everyone who is not just is not a believer, then no testimony would be rejected for fisq after examination, and it would not be permissible to ask about conditions to know justice and fisq; rather, one would ask about what faith he has and his ability to fulfill it, and his testimony would be accepted without asking about him and without regard to his conditions. And the consensus of the Community (ijma' al-umma) on examining conditions and not accepting simply what appears from the sayings — which are sufficient for matters whose conditions are faith such as permissibility, prohibition, inheritance, and then acts of worship — is evidence showing that faith and that by which a person becomes a believer and merits its rulings is not everything that negates the kinds of fisq (wickedness) and 'isyan (disobedience). There is no power except in God. And upon that the affair of this Community has proceeded from ...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage provides a scriptural and rational proof that faith (iman) does not disappear when a person loses justice ('adala). The author cites four Qur'anic verses. The first verse from Surat al-Ma'ida requires two just witnesses among the believers for a bequest. If every believer were necessarily just, the condition 'just' would be redundant—it would suffice to say 'two among you.' The fact that 'just' is specified shows that a believer may be just or unjust. The second verse from Surat al-Baqarah requires 'those you approve' as witnesses; if every believer were approved, no condition would be needed. The third verse from Surat al-Talaq again commands 'two just persons among you.' The fourth verse from Surat al-Nisa' requires 'maturity' (rushd) for handling property; it shows believers can be mature or not. Then the author presents a reductio ad absurdum: if the Mu'tazila position were true (that justice is inseparable from faith), then no testimony would ever be rejected for fisq after investigation, and judges would only ask about a person's faith declaration, not his moral character. But the actual practice (ijma') of the Muslim community is to investigate witnesses' moral conditions, proving that faith and justice are distinct. Faith itself is not the absence of all sins; a believer can commit fisq and 'isyan (disobedience) while retaining the name of faith. The passage concludes with the consensus of the community, indicating this has been the established practice.

Key Terms
العدالة
الفسق
الشهادة
الإيمان
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Justice ('adala) is an essential part of faith itself, so a believer cannot be unjust.
Correction: The Qur'anic verses and consensus show that a believer may be just or unjust; faith remains even with injustice.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would the legal practice be if the Mu'tazila position were true that faith requires justice?
A) Testimony would be accepted only from just believers
B) Testimony would be rejected after examination
C) Testimony would be accepted based solely on faith without investigating moral character
D) Only believers with visible piety could testify

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic evidence and community consensus demonstrate that faith does not require justice; a believer can be unjust yet remain a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith does not automatically make you just in your dealings, what does that mean for how you treat others and yourself? Are you relying on your iman as a free pass for your character flaws? This is a call to examine your own integrity separately from your religious identity.
?
The community examines witnesses' conditions, not just their faith. In your daily life, when you act unjustly, do you excuse yourself by saying 'I am a believer, so it's okay'? How can you ensure that your faith transforms your actions rather than becoming a shield for your wrongs?
?
The lesson shows that a believer can be unjust, but that does not diminish their faith in God. Which injustice in your own life have you been ignoring because you thought it didn't affect your standing as a believer? Confront it now and commit to change.

The Repentance Verse Proves Faith Remains with Major Sins
Maturidi uses Quran 66:8 to rebut the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quran 66:8 shows that major sinners are still called believers.

Core Idea
Allah addresses major sinners as believers, proving faith remains.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi begins his refutation of the Mu'tazila by citing a powerful verse: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah...' He points out that even though these people are commanded to repent for their sins, Allah still calls them believers. This simple observation undermines the claim that major sin removes the name of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi uses the verse to argue against the Mu'tazila.
Pay attention to the phrase 'O you who have believed' – it directly addresses sinners.
2
Identify the key point
What does the verse command? To whom is it addressed? How does this challenge the Mu'tazila?
3
Reflect on the implication
If faith were removed by major sin, Allah would not address sinners as believers. Think about why this argument is effective.
This is a straightforward textual proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض على المعتزلة ابتداء الآية وهو قوله : يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا ﴾ [التحريم، ٨/٦٦] ألزمهم التوبة وجعلها شرطًا للمغفرة، على إبقاء اسم الإيمان لهم، والصغائر مغفورة باجتناب الكبائر على قولهم. ثبت أن الآية في أصحاب الكبائر، وقد بقى لهم اسم الإيمان. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
والدليل على أن اسم الإيمان لا يزول بزوال العدالة قوله تعالى : يا أيها الذين آمنوا شهادة بينكم إذا حضر أحدكم الموت حين الوصية اثنان ذوا عدل منكم ﴾ [المائدة، ١٠٦/٥] الآية، ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلاً لكان يقول : اثنان منكم ، إذ كان ابتداء الآية في مخاطبة المؤمنين. ثبت أن قد يكون مؤمنًا عدلاً وغير عدل. وقال الله تعالى: يا أيها الذين آمنوا إذا تداينتم بدين إلى قوله ممن ترضون من الشهداء ﴾ [البقرة، (۲۸۲/۲] الآية، فلو كان كل مؤمن مرضيا لم يكن للشرط فائدة وكذلك قوله وأشهدوا ذوي عدل منكم ﴾ [الطلاق، ٢/٦٥] الآية . ثبت أن المؤمن يكون عدلاً وغير عدل وكذلك قوله: فإن آنستم منهم رشدا فدفعوا إليهم أموالهم ﴾ [النساء ، ٦/٤] الآية ، ثبت أنه قد يكون منهم رشيد وغير رشيد. ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلاً وكل من ليس بعدل ليس بمؤمن لكان لا شهادة تُرَدّ بالفسق بعد الامتحان ولا يجوز السؤال عن الأحوال ليعلم بها العدالة والفسق؛ بل على المكان الذي يُسأل عما عليه من الإيمان ويمكنه الوفاء [ في ذلك] ، فيجب قبول شهادته بلا سؤال عنه ولا اعتبار بأحواله وفي إجماع الأمة على الفحص عن الأحوال وترك النزول على ما يظهر من الأقوال - التي تكتفى بها فيما كان شرائطها الإيمان من الحل والحرمة والتوارث ثم العبادات - دليل يبيّن أن الإيمان وما به يصير المرء مؤمنًا ويستوجب أحكامه ليس هو كل ما ينفي أنواع الفسوق والعصيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى ذلك مضى أمر هذه الأمة من تعاهد الصلوات في الجماعات والصيام وإخراج الزكوات، على ما هم عليه من الاختلاف في هتك الحرمات والانهماك في المعاصي؛ ثبت بالذي عليه الأمة عدول المعتزلة والخوارج عن الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then what rebuts the Mu'tazila is the beginning of the verse, which is His statement: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance' [Q. 66:8]. He obligated repentance upon them and made it a condition for forgiveness, while keeping the name of faith for them, and minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins according to their claim. It is established that the verse concerns the people of major sins, and the name of faith remained for them. There is no power except with Allah.
And the proof that the name of faith does not disappear with the loss of justice is His statement: 'O you who have believed, testimony between you when death approaches one of you at the time of bequest, [let there be] two just men among you' [Q. 5:106]. If every believer were just, He would have said 'two among you,' since the beginning of the verse addresses the believers. It is established that one can be a believer just and not just. And Allah said: 'O you who have believed, when you contract a debt... to whom you are satisfied as witnesses' [Q. 2:282]. If every believer were satisfactory, the condition would have no benefit. Similarly, His statement: 'And call to witness two just men among you' [Q. 65:2]. It is established that the believer is just and not just. Similarly, His statement: 'If you perceive in them maturity, then release their property to them' [Q. 4:6]. It is established that among them there may be mature and immature. If every believer were just and everyone not just were not a believer, then no testimony would be rejected for immorality after examination, and it would not be permissible to ask about states to know justice and immorality. Rather, one would simply be asked about his faith and whether he can fulfill it, and his testimony would be accepted without questioning him or considering his states. And the consensus of the Ummah on investigating states and not relying on apparent statements — which suffice in matters whose conditions are faith, such as permissibility, prohibition, inheritance, and then acts of worship — is evidence showing that faith and what makes a person a believer and entitles him to its rulings are not everything that negates types of immorality and disobedience. There is no power except with Allah. Upon this the affair of this Ummah has continued in maintaining prayers in congregations, fasting, and giving alms, despite their differences in violating sanctities and indulging in sins. It is established by what the Ummah is upon that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have deviated from the truth. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and consensus-based argument that the name of faith (iman) does not leave a person who commits major sins. The author begins by refuting the Mu'tazila using the verse on repentance (Q. 66:8), noting that Allah addresses sinners as believers and commands them to repent, showing they remain believers despite sin. He then provides multiple Quranic proofs: Q. 5:106, 2:282, 65:2, and 4:6, each showing that believers can be unjust, unsatisfactory, or immature, thus faith and justice are distinct. Without this distinction, testimony procedures would be meaningless. The author grounds his argument in the consensus (ijma) of the Ummah, which investigates the moral states of witnesses rather than assuming justice from mere faith. This consensus demonstrates that faith does not automatically entail justice nor erase all sins. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have abandoned the truth by denying the name of faith to major sinners.

Key Terms
إيمان
توبة
كبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who commits a major sin ceases to be a believer.
Correction: The Quran addresses major sinners as 'believers' and commands them to repent, showing faith remains.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Quran 66:8 prove about major sinners?
A) They are no longer believers.
B) They are still called believers.
C) They must repent to become believers.
D) Their repentance is not accepted.

Key Takeaway
The verse of sincere repentance shows that major sinners retain the name of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you still consider yourself a believer? Why or why not? Let this verse challenge any doubt you may have about your own faith.
?
Allah calls you 'believer' even before you repent. How does that affect the way you think about your relationship with Him when you sin?
?
If the Mu'tazila were right, many Muslims would lose hope. Are there sins you avoid repenting from because you feel you have lost your faith? What does this verse say to you?

Faith and Disbelief

Faith Does Not Require Justice: Quranic Proofs and Consensus
Maturidi provides multiple Quranic proofs and consensus that faith does not entail justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how multiple Quranic verses and the consensus of the Ummah demonstrate that faith can exist without moral perfection.

Core Idea
Faith and justice are distinct; believers can be unjust.

Maturidi expands his argument by citing several Quranic verses that condition certain rulings on qualities like justice or maturity—qualities that not all believers possess. He then appeals to the consistent practice of the Ummah, which investigates witnesses' moral states, to show that faith alone does not guarantee justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the pattern: each verse sets a condition that some believers lack.
Key verses: 5:106, 2:282, 65:2, 4:6.
2
Examine each verse
For each verse, ask: What quality is required? Does the verse assume all believers have it?
For example, 'two just men' implies some believers are not just.
3
Understand the consensus argument
The Ummah investigates witnesses' states, which would be unnecessary if all believers were just.
4
Conclude the lesson
Faith and moral perfection are separate. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij are wrong.

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Repentance: The Two Interpretations and Three Objections
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's two interpretations of the repentance verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's attempt to reinterpret the repentance verse without acknowledging sin, and Maturidi's first two objections showing the impossibility and ingratitude of such interpretations.

Core Idea
Repentance requires a sin; pretending otherwise contradicts its meaning.

Imam Maturidi begins by noting that even though some groups perform acts of worship, they have deviated from truth due to their doctrines. He then presents al-Ka'bi's argument who tries to avoid the implication that believers have sins by suggesting repentance can be for minor sins or as a mere act of worship. Maturidi responds with three arguments, the first two of which we will examine here.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory remarks
Notice how Maturidi sets the stage: he accuses the Mu'tazila and Khawarij of deviating despite their worship.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's two ways
Find the two ways al-Ka'bi uses to interpret repentance: (1) repentance for minor sins already forgiven, (2) repentance as a voluntary act like tahalil.
3
Examine the first objection
Maturidi argues that the first way shows ignorance of repentance meaning regret for something done; it's impossible to regret something not done.
4
Examine the second objection
The second way implies ingratitude: if a sin is forgiven, repentance suggests it wasn't forgiven, which is thanklessness.
5
Consider the third objection
The third objection uses the verse 'perhaps your Lord will remove from you your misdeeds' to show that misdeeds exist and are removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعاهد الصلوات في الجماعات والصيام وإخراج الزكوات، على ما هم عليه من الاختلاف في هتك الحرمات والانهماك في المعاصي؛ ثبت بالذي عليه الأمة عدول المعتزلة والخوارج عن الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نذكر ما ذكر الكعبي فيما احتج عليه من القرآن من الحيل المستبعدة ليصرف عن نفسه وأتباعه اسم الإيمان ويوجب الإياس عن روح الله والاختيار لعدواته بكبيرة، كأنه به يحصل على نفع في الدنيا وحمد في الدين. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فقال لمن احتج عليه بقوله: يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا الآية، والتوبة لا تكون إلا عن ذنب بوجهين. أحدهما هو التوبة عن الصغيرة وإن كانت مغفورة؛ والثاني على التعبد كتكرار التهليل وكدعاء الملائكة بقوله: فاغفر للذين تابوا الآية. نقول له: الوجه الأول دل على جهله بمعنى التوبة، إذ هي الرجوع والندم، ومحال ذلك عما ليس عليه، وهو مغفور له لا يجوز عليه التعذيب. والثاني أن حق الله عليه إذا غفر له الحمد له والشكر على العفو وفي التوبة كفران ذلك، لأنه يوهم بقاءه بالتوبة. والثالث أنه قال: عسى ربكم أن يكفر عنكم سيئاتكم الآية، فجعله موقوفا على ما يكفره بالتوبة، ثبت أن الذنب باق وهو لم يزل مستحقا اسم الإيمان. والله أعلم. والتهليل له في كل وقت حكم التجديد، لأن حقيقة الأفعال أن لا يبقى، والتوبة يكون عن ذنب، ولا ذنب. وبعد، فإنه يجوز الأمر بالتهليل على التعبد ولا يجوز بالتوبة والاستغفار عن ذنب معفو مغفور، لما فيه إيهام أنه ليس بمغفور. وذلك كفران النعم، وغير جائز ذلك، كما لا يجوز الدعاء بأن لا يجور ولا يظلم. ودعاء الملائكة لما قد يكون لمن ذكر ذنوب غير مغفورة، فإلى ذلك ينصرف الدعاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
They are diligent in congregational prayers, fasting, and giving charity, despite their differences regarding violating sanctities and persisting in sins; it is established by what the community holds that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have deviated from the truth. And there is no power except through Allah. Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned in his argument from the Quran of far-fetched ruses to divert the name of faith from himself and his followers, and to cause despair of Allah's mercy and to choose enmity towards Him because of a major sin, as if by it he gains worldly benefit and praise in religion. And there is no power except through Allah. He said to one who argued against him with His saying: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance' (66:8), and repentance is only from a sin, in two ways. One of them is repentance from a minor sin even if it is forgiven; the second is as an act of worship like repeating the declaration of Allah's oneness and like the prayer of angels with His saying: 'So forgive those who repent' (40:7). We say to him: The first way indicates his ignorance of the meaning of repentance, for it is returning and regret, and that is impossible regarding something that is not upon him, and he is forgiven and punishment is not permissible upon him. The second is that the right of Allah upon him when He forgives him is praise and gratitude for pardon, and in repentance there is ingratitude for that, because it implies that he remains needing repentance. The third is that he said: 'Perhaps your Lord will remove from you your misdeeds' (66:8), and he made it conditional upon what He removes by repentance, so it is established that the sin remains and he still deserves the name of faith. And Allah knows best. The declaration of Allah's oneness has at every time the ruling of renewal, because the reality of actions is that they do not persist, and repentance is for a sin, and there is no sin. Moreover, commanding the declaration of Allah's oneness as an act of worship is permissible, but commanding repentance and seeking forgiveness for a pardoned and forgiven sin is not permissible, because it implies that it is not forgiven. That is ingratitude for blessings, and that is not permissible, just as it is not permissible to pray that He does not wrong or oppress. The prayer of the angels is for those who have unforgiven sins, and that is where the prayer is directed. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the repentance verse (66:8). The author first notes that despite the acts of worship performed by the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, they have deviated from the truth. Then he presents al-Ka'bi's argument: he tries to reinterpret the verse to deny that the addressees have sins, suggesting that repentance can be for minor sins or as an act of worship. Maturidi responds with three objections: (1) The first interpretation shows ignorance of repentance, which is regret for a committed sin; it is impossible to regret something not done. (2) The second interpretation implies ingratitude, because if a sin is already forgiven, repentance suggests it was not forgiven. (3) The third point uses the verse's phrase 'perhaps your Lord will remove from you your misdeeds,' which implies the misdeeds exist and are removed by repentance, so the sin remains before repentance. The author then discusses the tahalil (declaration of oneness) and istighfar (seeking forgiveness): tahalil can be done as an act of worship at any time, but repentance and istighfar for a forgiven sin are not permissible because they imply the sin is not forgiven. He compares this to praying that Allah does not wrong, which is improper. The angels' prayer is for those with actual unforgiven sins. Thus, al-Ka'bi's attempt to remove the name of faith from the sinner is refuted.

Key Terms
التوبة
الإيمان
الكبيرة
الندم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance can be performed as a worship even if no sin exists.
Correction: Repentance is fundamentally regret and turning back from a sin; it cannot be sincere without an actual sin.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the problem with al-Ka'bi's claim that repentance can be performed as an act of worship for a forgiven sin?
A) It is permissible because it increases devotion.
B) It implies ingratitude by suggesting the sin was not forgiven.
C) It is an innovation in religion.
D) It contradicts the consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's attempt to separate repentance from sin fails because repentance is inherently tied to regret for a committed offense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last sinned, did you genuinely regret it? Or did you simply say 'Astaghfirullah' without feeling remorse? Al-Ka'bi's error was to separate repentance from real sin. Have you ever treated repentance as a routine phrase rather than a heartfelt return? Commit today to only say 'I repent' when you truly mean it.
?
Maturidi warns that repentance without a prior sin is ingratitude if the sin is already forgiven. This shows that taking Allah's forgiveness for granted is dangerous. Do you ever delay repentance, assuming Allah will forgive anyway? How can you break that habit starting now?
?
Al-Ka'bi reinterpreted the Quran to avoid admitting his own sinfulness. Are there areas in your life where you twist excuses to avoid facing your faults? Write down one excuse you use and replace it with honest repentance.

Maturidi on the Permissibility of Tahalil vs. Repentance for Forgiven Sins
Maturidi distinguishes between permissible repetition of tahalil and impermissible repentance for forgiven sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repeating the declaration of oneness (tahalil) is permissible at any time, whereas repentance and seeking forgiveness for a forgiven sin is not allowed, and how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's analogy.

Core Idea
Repentance for a forgiven sin implies ingratitude; tahalil remains a valid worship.

Having refuted al-Ka'bi's two interpretations, Maturidi now addresses a further point: al-Ka'bi had compared repentance to the repetition of the declaration of oneness (tahalil) as a voluntary act. Maturidi distinguishes between the two: tahalil can be repeated anytime because actions do not persist, but repentance is for a sin. Commanding repentance for a forgiven sin implies it was not forgiven, which is ingratitude. He also clarifies the angels' prayer for those who repent refers to those with actual unforgiven sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tahalil
Notice that Maturidi states that tahalil has the ruling of renewal at every time because actions do not persist.
2
Contrast with repentance
He argues that repentance and istighfar are only for sins; commanding them for a forgiven sin implies the sin is not forgiven, which is ingratitude.
3
Consider the analogy with prayer
Maturidi gives an analogy: it is not permissible to pray that Allah does not wrong, because it implies the possibility of injustice.
4
Understand the angels' prayer
The angels' prayer for forgiveness is for those who have actual unforgiven sins, not for those already forgiven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعاهد الصلوات في الجماعات والصيام وإخراج الزكوات، على ما هم عليه من الاختلاف في هتك الحرمات والانهماك في المعاصي؛ ثبت بالذي عليه الأمة عدول المعتزلة والخوارج عن الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نذكر ما ذكر الكعبي فيما احتج عليه من القرآن من الحيل المستبعدة ليصرف عن نفسه وأتباعه اسم الإيمان ويوجب الإياس عن روح الله والاختيار لعدواته بكبيرة، كأنه به يحصل على نفع في الدنيا وحمد في الدين. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فقال لمن احتج عليه بقوله: يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا الآية، والتوبة لا تكون إلا عن ذنب بوجهين. أحدهما هو التوبة عن الصغيرة وإن كانت مغفورة؛ والثاني على التعبد كتكرار التهليل وكدعاء الملائكة بقوله: فاغفر للذين تابوا الآية. نقول له: الوجه الأول دل على جهله بمعنى التوبة، إذ هي الرجوع والندم، ومحال ذلك عما ليس عليه، وهو مغفور له لا يجوز عليه التعذيب. والثاني أن حق الله عليه إذا غفر له الحمد له والشكر على العفو وفي التوبة كفران ذلك، لأنه يوهم بقاءه بالتوبة. والثالث أنه قال: عسى ربكم أن يكفر عنكم سيئاتكم الآية، فجعله موقوفا على ما يكفره بالتوبة، ثبت أن الذنب باق وهو لم يزل مستحقا اسم الإيمان. والله أعلم. والتهليل له في كل وقت حكم التجديد، لأن حقيقة الأفعال أن لا يبقى، والتوبة يكون عن ذنب، ولا ذنب. وبعد، فإنه يجوز الأمر بالتهليل على التعبد ولا يجوز بالتوبة والاستغفار عن ذنب معفو مغفور، لما فيه إيهام أنه ليس بمغفور. وذلك كفران النعم، وغير جائز ذلك، كما لا يجوز الدعاء بأن لا يجور ولا يظلم. ودعاء الملائكة لما قد يكون لمن ذكر ذنوب غير مغفورة، فإلى ذلك ينصرف الدعاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
They are diligent in congregational prayers, fasting, and giving charity, despite their differences regarding violating sanctities and persisting in sins; it is established by what the community holds that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have deviated from the truth. And there is no power except through Allah. Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned in his argument from the Quran of far-fetched ruses to divert the name of faith from himself and his followers, and to cause despair of Allah's mercy and to choose enmity towards Him because of a major sin, as if by it he gains worldly benefit and praise in religion. And there is no power except through Allah. He said to one who argued against him with His saying: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance' (66:8), and repentance is only from a sin, in two ways. One of them is repentance from a minor sin even if it is forgiven; the second is as an act of worship like repeating the declaration of Allah's oneness and like the prayer of angels with His saying: 'So forgive those who repent' (40:7). We say to him: The first way indicates his ignorance of the meaning of repentance, for it is returning and regret, and that is impossible regarding something that is not upon him, and he is forgiven and punishment is not permissible upon him. The second is that the right of Allah upon him when He forgives him is praise and gratitude for pardon, and in repentance there is ingratitude for that, because it implies that he remains needing repentance. The third is that he said: 'Perhaps your Lord will remove from you your misdeeds' (66:8), and he made it conditional upon what He removes by repentance, so it is established that the sin remains and he still deserves the name of faith. And Allah knows best. The declaration of Allah's oneness has at every time the ruling of renewal, because the reality of actions is that they do not persist, and repentance is for a sin, and there is no sin. Moreover, commanding the declaration of Allah's oneness as an act of worship is permissible, but commanding repentance and seeking forgiveness for a pardoned and forgiven sin is not permissible, because it implies that it is not forgiven. That is ingratitude for blessings, and that is not permissible, just as it is not permissible to pray that He does not wrong or oppress. The prayer of the angels is for those who have unforgiven sins, and that is where the prayer is directed. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the repentance verse (66:8). The author first notes that despite the acts of worship performed by the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, they have deviated from the truth. Then he presents al-Ka'bi's argument: he tries to reinterpret the verse to deny that the addressees have sins, suggesting that repentance can be for minor sins or as an act of worship. Maturidi responds with three objections: (1) The first interpretation shows ignorance of repentance, which is regret for a committed sin; it is impossible to regret something not done. (2) The second interpretation implies ingratitude, because if a sin is already forgiven, repentance suggests it was not forgiven. (3) The third point uses the verse's phrase 'perhaps your Lord will remove from you your misdeeds,' which implies the misdeeds exist and are removed by repentance, so the sin remains before repentance. The author then discusses the tahalil (declaration of oneness) and istighfar (seeking forgiveness): tahalil can be done as an act of worship at any time, but repentance and istighfar for a forgiven sin are not permissible because they imply the sin is not forgiven. He compares this to praying that Allah does not wrong, which is improper. The angels' prayer is for those with actual unforgiven sins. Thus, al-Ka'bi's attempt to remove the name of faith from the sinner is refuted.

Key Terms
التوبة
التهليل
الاستغفار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance can be performed like tahalil as a general act of worship.
Correction: Repentance is intrinsically linked to sin; without sin, it is improper and implies ingratitude.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible to command repentance for a pardoned sin?
A) Because it is an innovation.
B) Because it implies ingratitude.
C) Because it contradicts the Quran.
D) Because angels do not pray for them.

Key Takeaway
Repentance is meaningful only when there is a sin; commanding it for a forgiven sin implies ingratitude, unlike the perpetual worship of tahalil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever repeated 'Astaghfirullah' mechanically without an actual sin, thinking it always brings reward? How will you now ensure your repentance is sincere and tied to real transgressions?
?
Maturidi says commanding repentance for a forgiven sin implies ingratitude—like praying that Allah does not wrong. Is there any area in your life where you act as if Allah might be unjust, instead of trusting His perfect forgiveness and mercy?
?
The angels pray specifically for those with unforgiven sins. Are you delaying repentance for a known sin, hoping the angels' prayer covers you? What concrete step will you take today to seek forgiveness for that specific sin?

Refuting al-Ka'bi on Quranic Rebuke
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the verse 'Why do you say what you do not do' (61:2).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that Quranic rebuke can be for future actions only, and why this matters for the definition of faith.

Core Idea
Quranic rebuke addresses real faults, not hypothetical futures.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi responds to a Mu'tazili scholar, al-Ka'bi, who argued that the verse "Why do you say what you do not do" (61:2) does not imply actual sin by the addressees, but is a precautionary warning. Maturidi dismantles this interpretation, showing that it leads to absurdity and undermines divine justice. As you read, pay attention to how Maturidi turns the argument back on al-Ka'bi, exposing the logical and theological flaws in his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the sharp rebuke tone in Maturidi's words.
The Arabic uses strong rhetorical language, e.g., 'فهنيئاً لك' (congratulations to you) sarcastically.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim and Maturidi's counter
Find where al-Ka'bi's interpretation is presented: he says the verse is for something not yet done. Then trace Maturidi's response, first about intention, then about foolishness.
3
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why Maturidi insists that rebuke implies actual sin. How would al-Ka'bi's view affect the concept of faith and divine justice?
This connects to the larger debate on whether major sins remove faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال في قوله: يا أيها الذين آمنوا لم تقولون ما لا تفعلون (الصف، 2/61): إلا أن ذا يكون فيما لم يكن فعله، كمن يرى آخر يدعوه بعض الغواة إلى أمر قبيح، فيقول على سبيل النهي: لم تفعل يا أخي ما ينقص دينك ويوجب عليك سخط ربك؟ لا أنه فعله، لكن لئلا يفعله. فيقال له: إن كان جهدك في صرف اسم الإيمان عن المبتلى بكبيرة ـ على ما فيه من تعظيم الرحمن وخشية العواقب - لئلا تُسمى أنت به فلك ما اخترت في نفسك وسويته؛ وإن كان ذلك لتزيل هذا عن غيرك فهو يعلم جرأتك في ذلك على الله بما فهم من تسمية الله إياه بغير الذي سميته، فلا يُحتمل أن يرتاب في خبر الله ـ مع ما يعلم من نفسه كذلك ـ بافترائك على الله بتسويل الشيطان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الذي ذكرته لا يحتمله إلا سفيه: أن يقول ويعاتب على ما يعلم كذبه فيما يعاتبه عليه. فأما الله سبحانه الذي لا يخفى عليه شيء يتعالى عن الرتبة التي يأنف منها كل ذي لب. والله المستعان. وأما أنت فحقيق لذلك، لأنك تيأس به من روح الله وتؤثر بشهوتك عداوة الله وولاية الشيطان وتتعرض في مذهبه لمقته ولعنه. فهنيئاً لك ما اخترته لنفسك عند الكريم والرحيم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said regarding His saying: 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do' (61:2): except that this is about something he has not done, like one who sees another being called by some deceivers to a reprehensible matter, and he says as a prohibition: 'Why have you done, O my brother, what diminishes your religion and incurs your Lord's wrath upon you?' Not that he did it, but so that he does not do it. It is said to him: If your effort is to divert the name of faith from the one afflicted with a major sin—given the magnification of the Merciful and fear of consequences—so that you are not called by it, then you have what you chose for yourself and deemed equal; if that is to remove this from others, then he knows your audacity in that against Allah from what he understood of Allah's naming him with other than what you named him, so it is not conceivable that he would doubt Allah's report—while he knows of himself likewise—because of your slander against Allah by the whispering of Satan. And there is no power except through Allah. Then what you mentioned cannot be borne except by a fool: that he says and rebukes regarding what he knows to be false in what he rebukes him for. As for Allah, the Exalted, from whom nothing is hidden, He is exalted above the rank that every intelligent person would disdain. And Allah is the source of help. And as for you, you deserve that, because you cause despair thereby of Allah's mercy and prefer by your desire the enmity of Allah and the allegiance of Satan, and you expose yourself in his doctrine to His wrath and curse. So congratulations to you for what you have chosen for yourself from the Generous and Merciful. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the verse 'Why do you say what you do not do' (61:2). Al-Ka'bi claimed that this verse does not imply that the addressees actually committed a sin; rather, it is a prohibition against a future action, similar to warning someone against something they have not yet done. Maturidi responds sharply. He first argues that if al-Ka'bi's goal is to remove the name of faith from the one who commits a major sin in order to avoid being called a sinner himself, then he is free to choose that for himself. But if he intends to remove the name of faith from others, then the addressee knows his own state and knows that Allah named him a believer despite his sins; thus he cannot doubt Allah's report. Maturidi then says that such a speech can only come from a fool—one who rebukes someone for something he knows to be false. Allah, who knows all, is far above such absurdity. He ends with a severe admonition: al-Ka'bi's view leads to despair of Allah's mercy and preference for enmity with Allah and alliance with Satan, deserving wrath and curse.

الإيمان
الكبيرة
الرد
الافتراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret Quranic rebuke as merely cautionary for future actions, not implying an existing fault.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this interpretation is foolish; rebuke in the Quran addresses actual sins, and reinterpreting it as hypothetical leads to slander against Allah and doubt in His report.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi characterize al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the verse 'Why do you say what you do not do'?
A) A valid rhetorical device.
B) A wise attempt to protect faith from slander.
C) A foolish statement that only a fool would accept.
D) A necessary correction of a common misconception.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi forcefully defends the principle that Quranic rebuke addresses actual deeds, and reinterpreting it as a hypothetical warning leads to theological absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever reinterpreted a clear Quranic command to avoid feeling convicted about a sin in your life? What would it take for you to submit to Allah's rebuke without making excuses?
?
Are you more concerned with protecting your theological label (e.g., Ash'ari, Maturidi, Salafi) than with admitting a mistake in your understanding? How does this passage expose the danger of defending a position that contradicts Allah's report?

Refuting al-Ka'bi's Argument from the Verse of Khushu'
Maturidi begins refuting al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the khushu' verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects al-Ka'bi's claim that the verse of khushu' (57:16) separates faith from inner humility.

Core Idea
Faith without humility is not faith; the verse rebukes believers, not redefines faith.

Al-Ka'bi, a prominent theologian, argued that Quran 57:16 proves that the name 'believer' can be applied even when the heart lacks humility, thus reducing faith to mere verbal affirmation and knowledge. Maturidi begins his refutation by pointing out the logical flaw in this reading, showing that it strips faith of its essential inner dimension. This passage presents the opening of his critique, though the argument is cut short.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's argument
Understand al-Ka'bi's interpretation: he claims the verse addresses 'those who have believed' while stating their hearts are not submissive, so faith is independent of khushu'.
Al-Ka'bi's view reduces faith to affirmation and knowledge alone.
2
Examine Maturidi's first point
Maturidi says al-Ka'bi's interpretation 'affirms the name of faith without humility' — a contradiction because faith inherently includes humility in the Maturidi view.
Maturidi points out al-Ka'bi's opponents require both affirmation and knowledge, unlike his sparse definition.
3
Consider the incomplete second point
The text begins a second point about the verse possibly referring to those who fear Allah, but it is cut off. Reflect on what Maturidi might have intended.
The incomplete argument suggests a contextual interpretation.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This debate shows how a single verse can be used to support radically different definitions of faith — a central issue in Islamic theology.
Understanding this refutation clarifies the Ash'ari/Maturidi insistence on inner states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال في قوله تعالى: ألم يأن للذين آمنوا أن تخشع قلوبهم لذكر الله أنه أثبت اسم الإيمان وإن لم تخشع قلوبهم: إن أوّل ذلك إثبات اسم الإيمان بلا خشوع، وأنتم لا ترون إلا التصديق باللسان والمعرفة بالقلب. والثاني أن قد يقول لمن يخشى الله ويقوم بالغاية في...
Then he said regarding His saying, the Exalted: 'Has it not come time for those who have believed that their hearts should become humbly submissive to the remembrance of Allah' (57:16) that He affirmed the name of faith even if their hearts are not submissive: The first of that is affirming the name of faith without humility, and you see nothing but affirmation by the tongue and knowledge by the heart. The second is that He may say to one who fears Allah and stands at the utmost extent in...
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a refutation of al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the verse (57:16) about khushu' (humble submission). Al-Ka'bi argued that this verse proves that believers can be called believers even without khushu' in their hearts, because Allah addresses them as 'those who have believed' while stating that their hearts have not yet become submissive. Maturidi points out that this interpretation leads to affirming the name of faith without any humility, which contradicts the view of al-Ka'bi's opponents who require both verbal affirmation and inner knowledge. Then Maturidi begins to present a second point, but the text cuts off. The incomplete sentence suggests he was explaining that the verse may be addressed to those who already fear Allah and strive, but the argument is not fully given here.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Quran 57:16 proves that a person can be a complete believer without any humility or submission in the heart.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse rebukes believers for lacking khushūʿ; it does not define faith as excluding khushūʿ. The address 'those who have believed' refers to their initial faith, not a permanent state.

Checkpoint

A) That believers must always have khushu'.
B) That the name of faith can be affirmed even without khushu'.
C) That khushu' is impossible for believers.
D) That the verse abrogates previous rulings.

Key Takeaway
The verse of khushu' does not separate faith from inner humility; rather, it highlights a deficiency in believers, and Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's attempt to strip faith of its essential inner component.

What would it take for you to truly integrate faith and humility?
?
Are you satisfied with a faith that lacks the trembling of the heart before Allah's words? If the verse is a rebuke, what is it rebuking in your own life? What step will you take today to ensure your heart is not merely affirming but submissive?

Response to Claim that Grave Sinners Are Enemies
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite claim on grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that grave sinners should be cursed rather than prayed for.

Core Idea
Reform is prayer for good, so even a grave sinner deserves prayers for reform.

The Mu'tazilites held that committing a major sin makes one an enemy of Allah, deserving of cursing and not prayer. Imam Maturidi counters by defining reform as prayer for good, implying that praying for a sinner is an act of reform, not opposition to Allah. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation carefully.
Notice the structure: objection followed by response.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim (enmity, no prayer, cursing) and Maturidi's implicit refutation (reform = prayer for good).
3
Reflect on the key phrase
Focus on the phrase 'وما الإصلاح إلا الدعاء بالخير والصلاح' – what does it imply about the permissibility of prayer?
Consider how this refutes the claim by redefining the act of prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم على قوله إن صاحب الكبيرة عدو الله لا يسع له الدعاء بالخير ويلزم لعنه، وما الإصلاح إلا الدعاء بالخير والصلاح ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then regarding his saying that the committer of a major sin is an enemy of Allah, it is not permissible to pray for good for him, and cursing him is obligatory, and reform is nothing but prayer for good and righteousness, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response. The Mu'tazilite claims that a grave sinner is an enemy of Allah, so praying for him is impermissible and cursing is obligatory. The author implicitly refutes this by stating that reform (الإصلاح) is essentially prayer for good and righteousness, implying that such prayer is not forbidden. The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a declaration of reliance on Allah.

Key Terms

عدو الله
دعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Grave sinners are enemies of Allah and must be cursed, not prayed for.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prayer for good is reform, which is permissible even for grave sinners.

Checkpoint

B) It is obligatory to curse him instead.
C) It is permissible as part of seeking reform.
D) It is only allowed if he repents.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite view by asserting that reform is prayer for good, so praying for a grave sinner is an act of reform, not a transgression.
